Classification:

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 3
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day shows drastically reduced consumption compared to the reference Friday (mean 64.54 vs 167.51), with clear evidence of manipulation. The ratio between the query day and the reference day varies across samples (e.g., ~0.39 at midnight, ~0.29 at 7 AM, ~0.39 at 8 PM), indicating a per‑sample scaling factor rather than a constant multiplier. This rules out Attack 1 (constant factor) and Attack 2/5/6/7/9/10/12/IEEE (zero segments, random average‑based values, cut‑offs, flat profiles, or pattern reversal). Attack 4 (theft over a random period) is less specific; if the whole day were selected, it would behave like Attack 1, not explain the sample‑wise variation observed here. The per‑sample random scaling is the defining characteristic of Attack 3.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Each 15‑minute sample of the actual consumption is multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. In the queried day, this results in a non‑uniform reduction where some intervals (e.g., morning dip) are scaled more severely than others (e.g., evening peak), preserving the general daily pattern but with varying degrees of suppression.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is significantly reduced overall—the daily mean drops from ~167 to ~64, and the minimum falls from ~96 to ~31. The trend is altered because the random per‑sample multipliers introduce distortions; the overall diurnal shape is recognizable but less consistent than the original.
- Possible technical causes: False data injection (manipulating the communication stream with randomly scaled values), physical tampering such as magnetic interference or reverse current that introduces non‑linear measurement errors, or a partial meter bypass that intermittently reduces the recorded consumption in a non‑uniform manner.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. Let's narrow down. Attack 0: all zeros. No. Attack 1: constant multiplier. Not constant. Attack 2: on-off zeros. No zeros. Attack 3: variable multiplier. Could be. Attack 4: reduction over a selected period. If the period is the whole day, then all measurements reduced, but they could be reduced by different factors? The description: "All measurements in that period are reduced." Not specific. Could be constant subtraction, or multiplication? In many NTL datasets, attack 4 is a random percentage reduction over a contiguous period. I recall that in some works, attack 4 is a partial reduction attack where for a certain duration, consumption is multiplied by a factor between 0 and 1, constant for that period. That would create a day where a segment is scaled down uniformly, while other segments are normal. Here, the entire day seems scaled, but not uniformly. Given the data, the most likely attack is that the meter has been tampered to report a fraction of the actual consumption, but the fraction varies over time, i.e., Attack 3. Attack IEEE: some intervals zero, some actual. Not the case.

Thus, Attack 3 is the best match. Now, attack 5 was considered and ruled out because pattern is not completely random; it retains some of the original shape. Attack 1 ruled out because reduction factor not constant. Attack 4 would be if only part of day reduced, but here entire day reduced.

Attack 3 is the only one that explicitly applies per-sample random scaling, which explains the variable ratio while preserving approximate shape. So I'll go with Attack 3.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

So if it's Attack 1, all samples would be multiplied by exactly the same factor. The fact that the ratio varies suggests it's not a constant factor. So Attack 1 is ruled out.

Attack 3 uses a unique factor per sample, which is exactly what we see. So Attack 3 is most likely.

Attack 4 would only affect a random period, not the entire day uniformly reduced. Attack 1 would have constant scaling factor. The consumption pattern follows the normal shape but with sample-specific scaling factors, indicative of multiplicative tampering with varying strength per reading. Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Attack 5 would produce a noise-like pattern, not a clear diurnal pattern. Here there is a clear low and peak.

Attack 6: cut-off. Let's see if there is a cut-off value that many points hit. The max is 29.06, but only one occurrence. Other high values: 28.49, 26.21, 25.07, 26.21, 29.06, 24.50... Not many at exactly the same value. So unlikely.

Attack 7: subtract cut-off, negatives become zero. There are no zeros.

Thus, Attack 3 is the most probable.

The reference week has means between 18.93 and 24.36, and the same weekday (Wednesday) is 24.36. A drop to 14.95 is a 38% reduction. Moreover, the min is 7.41 vs 11.94. The reference Day 3 has cal_month=4, and query day has cal_month=5, dom=17, so about a month later. Seasonal change might cause some reduction, but typically not such a drastic drop (from mean 24 to 15) in a month, and the pattern would likely shift in timing. Also, the std is lower, indicating less variability, which could be from scaling. And the overall shape is preserved. So this is almost certainly manipulated.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. 3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Output Format:
Classification:
- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  (fill only if manipulated)
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes

Actually, Day 0: dow=6 (Saturday?), Day 1: dow=0 (Sunday?), Day 2: dow=1 (Monday?), Day 3: dow=2 (Tuesday?), Day 4: dow=3 (Wednesday?), Day 5: dow=4 (Thursday?), Day 6: dow=5 (Friday?). The query day has dow=1, which matches Day 2 in the week (Monday). However, the dom (day of month) for query day is 9, while in the reference week, Day 2 dom=2. So it's a different Monday. The query day is from a different week, but same month and same weekday (Monday). So we should compare the query day pattern to the typical Monday pattern from the reference week (Day 2) and also to the overall week pattern to see if it's consistent.

Compare patterns:
Reference Monday: starts high (~65), then drops gradually to around 30-40 range, then peaks again to 80s in the evening. The shape is a typical U-shape or maybe two peaks: morning high, then low, then evening high.
Query day: starts moderate (~37-39), dips to ~25-32 range, then rises to a peak of 61 in the late evening (around readings 81-84), then drops. The shape is somewhat similar: there's a period of low consumption and then an increase in the evening. However, the magnitude is consistently lower. For example, early morning: reference had 65, 56, 55, 51,... while query has 37, 36, 38, 39... That's roughly half to two-thirds. Midday lows: reference had 30-40 range, query has 25-32 range. Evening peak: reference peaked at 82.52, query peak is 61.37. So the query looks like a scaled-down version of the reference Monday? Let's see if it's a constant scaling. Compute ratio between query and reference for some points. But reference day is not perfectly aligned time-wise? The patterns are both 96 readings. They correspond to the same weekday but different date, so we can compare pointwise if the consumption behavior is similar. The shape of the query day: starts around 37-39, dips to 25-32, then rises to 61. The reference day starts at 65, dips to 30-33, peaks at 82. So the ratio isn't constant. The query day has a less pronounced morning peak. It might be Attack 1 (multiply by constant random factor between 0 and 1) or Attack 3 (different random factors per sample). But note: Attack 1 applies a single constant factor to the entire day; Attack 3 applies different factors per sample. The query day's variation (std=7.07) is lower than the reference day's std (11.46), which could be due to scaling. If Attack 1 with a constant factor, the trend remains same, magnitude uniformly reduced. That would mean all values are multiplied by some factor, say around 0.6-0.7? But the min is 25.42 vs 30.10 (factor ~0.84) not exactly consistent. The max is 61.37 vs 82.52 (factor ~0.74). The mean is 35.69 vs 51.81 (factor ~0.69). The factor varies. So it's not a single constant factor; it seems like a variable factor. That points to Attack 3: "actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." That would cause varying reduction, possibly changing trend and amplitude. Alternatively, Attack 4: randomly selected period where measurements are reduced. But the query day doesn't show a single period of reduction; it seems reduced throughout the entire day, but with varying degree. Attack 2: on-off attack, replacing samples with zeros for random durations. No zeros here, all values are non-zero. Attack 0: all zeros, not the case. Attack 5: each sample replaced by false value obtained by multiplying average normal consumption by random factor. That would yield a pattern completely different from original, unlikely to follow the same temporal shape. But here, the query day still has a rough daily pattern: lower in the middle, higher in the evening. It resembles the reference Monday shape but compressed. Attack 6: cut-off point, replacing values above cut-off with cut-off. Here, the peak is 61, but there's no evidence of a hard ceiling because the evening values rise to 61 but earlier values are lower. Could be a cut-off around 60? But the reference day had many values above 61. If there was a cut-off around 61, then all values above 61 would be replaced by 61. That could explain why the max is 61.37 (close to 61? Actually 61.37 is above 61). But the cut-off is a random point, and if it's 61.37, then values above that would be replaced. In the query data, there's a value 61.37 itself, so that could be a cut-off threshold? However, look at the reference day: many values in the evening are above 61.37 (like 66.99, 71.84, 76.70, etc.). If Attack 6 with cut-off around 61, those would all become 61. But in query day, we see a gradual increase to 61.37, then decrease. The values around the peak are 57.03, 61.37, 60.13, 52.07, etc. That doesn't look like a hard ceiling (flat). So Attack 6 less likely. Attack 7: subtract a cut-off point, negative becomes zero. Could produce shape similar to original but shifted down. If we subtract a constant, the shape remains but shifted. For example, if we subtract ~20-30 from all reference values, then values below the cut-off become zero? But query day has no zeros, so subtraction might not cause zeros. However, the min is 25.42, so if we subtracted a large value, many would be zero. Not the case. So Attack 7 unlikely.

Attack 10: reverse pattern, not the case because pattern isn't reversed. Attack 12: replace with lower-consuming user's data. Could be, but we don't have that data. Attack IEEE: on-off across 15-min intervals, some zeroed out. No zeros, so no.

So the most likely are Attack 1 (constant scaling) or Attack 3 (variable scaling). The varying degree of reduction suggests Attack 3 more than Attack 1. Attack 3 specifies "different random factors between 0 and 1, unique factor per sample". The query day's pattern is similar to the reference Monday but with a compressed magnitude and some flattening (std lower). That fits: original trend somewhat preserved but amplitude changed, and trend may be modified because different factors distort the shape. Example: early morning values are scaled by maybe ~0.6, midday scaled by ~0.8, evening scaled by ~0.75. That's variable.

Could it be Attack 4? Attack 4: randomly selected period where all measurements are reduced. The query day looks reduced all day, not just a period. So probably not Attack 4.

We must also consider if it's an original day. The mean 35.69 is significantly lower than any day in the reference week. The reference week days all have means around 51-59. Even the lowest day in the reference week (Day 2) has mean 51.81. The query day's mean is 35.69, far lower. The max is also much lower. So it is highly likely manipulated. However, could it be a day with genuinely lower consumption? Possible, but the deviation is substantial. Furthermore, the shape is similar to Monday, just scaled down. That suggests an attack that scales down consumption, likely to steal electricity. So classification: Manipulated.

Now, identified attack: I'm leaning toward Attack 3. But let's double-check Attack 1: "Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1)". If the factor were constant, the pattern would be exactly scaled. Is it exactly scaled? Compute ratio of query to reference for each point? But the query day and reference Monday are not perfectly aligned because it's a different week; the customer's consumption might not be exactly identical timing. However, the patterns are broadly similar. The variation in scaling suggests it's not a single constant. Attack 3: "actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." That matches the varying degree of reduction.

Attack 6 also possible if the cut-off value is around 61 and the attacker enforces a ceiling. But as argued, no flat ceiling visible. Attack IEEE: would have zeros, none seen.

Alternative considered: Attack 1 (constant scaling) - but variability in reduction factor across the day makes constant scaling less likely; Attack 4 (period reduction) - but reduction is throughout rather than a specific period.

Confidence: Medium, because we don't have the exact original day data for that specific day, and it's possible the customer genuinely changed behavior. But given the magnitude difference and the similarity in shape, I'd say manipulated.

- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is significantly reduced (mean drops from ~52 to ~36, max from ~82 to ~61). The trend is partially preserved but altered: the morning rise is heavily dampened, the midday trough is deepened, and the evening peak is lowered and less pronounced.

That would produce a pattern that is a noisy version of the original, where the shape is preserved but with random perturbations. That could be consistent with Attack 3 if the random factors are somewhat consistent? Actually, if you multiply each sample by a random factor, you'd introduce additional variance? Not necessarily: if the factors are independent, the variance of the scaled series would be the variance of the product. But the std is reduced proportionally to the mean reduction, so the coefficient of variation (std/mean) might be similar. Let's check: Reference Day 2 CV = 5.01/11.53 ≈ 0.434. Query day CV = 2.84/6.93 ≈ 0.410. Slightly lower but close. So it's plausible that the original was scaled down uniformly or nearly uniformly.

Attack 4: randomly selected period within the observation window where measurements are reduced. This would produce a day where a contiguous block of time has reduced consumption, while the rest is normal. The query day shows all values are reduced; no block looks normal. The max is only 14.30, while reference max is 25.32, so the entire day is low. So Attack 4 is unlikely.

Attack 5: each sample replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1. That would produce a random pattern centered around a constant, not following the typical time-of-day shape. The query day clearly has a time-of-day pattern, not random scatter. So Attack 5 is out.

Attack 6: selects a cut-off point and replaces all values above it with that cut-off. So the max would be clipped to that cut-off, and many values would be exactly the cut-off. In the query day, we see many distinct values and no obvious repeated ceiling value. The max is 14.30, but there is only one value at 14.30? Let's check the list: ... 14.30, 12.20, 11.85, ... So not many repeated max. There are repeated values like 8.02 appears many times, but that's likely natural. So Attack 6 seems unlikely.

Attack 7: subtracts a cut-off point from each sample; if negative, zero. That would reduce all values by a constant, but the min is 2.44, not zero, and the shape would be shifted down. The query day overnight values are very low (2.44) but not zero. If we assume a constant shift, subtract 2.5 from reference Day 2 overnight (~5.7) gets ~3.2, close. But then daytime peak 25.32 - 2.5 = 22.82, not 14.30. So not a constant subtraction.

Attack 12: replaces consumption with that of a lower-consuming user. That would result in a completely different pattern, potentially with different timing. The query day still has a pattern similar to the typical household: low overnight, morning rise, evening peak. So it's likely the same household but scaled down.

So the most plausible attacks are Attack 1 (constant scaling factor) or Attack 3 (varying scaling factors). Since the scaling factor appears not perfectly constant (overnight ratio ~0.48, daytime ~0.56), Attack 3 is more likely. But we need to check if the differences are statistically significant or just due to normal week-to-week variation. The reference week days also show variation: for instance, Day 0 min 3.80, max 27.85; Day 6 min 5.06, max 24.68. So the actual normal consumption for that specific Tuesday in May might have been lower overall due to weather or occupancy. However, the mean dropped from ~11.5 to 6.93, a 40% reduction, which is substantial. Also, the max dropped from ~25 to 14.3, a 43% reduction. The min dropped from ~5.7 to 2.44, a 57% reduction. So overnight reduction is larger. That could be consistent with Attack 3 if the random factors happen to be smaller for lower values, but Attack 3 uses random factors per sample, so they should be independent of magnitude. But maybe the attacker used a factor that varies but is generally lower at night? That could happen by chance. Alternatively, Attack 1 with a constant factor of around 0.5-0.6 would give a uniform scaling; the differences in ratio could be due to the specific shape of the day not exactly matching the reference Tuesday, because the query day is a different Tuesday. So we need to see if the pattern is consistent with a scaled version of a typical day. Let's examine the shape of the query day: It has a morning ramp-up starting around index 30? 2.44 min at index 27? Let's map indices to time: 96 intervals per day, so each interval is 15 minutes. Index 0 is 00:00-00:15. So overnight low is around indices 20-30 (5:00-7:30). Then it starts rising: indices 33-35: 4.53, 4.88, 7.32, 8.02... That's around 8:15-8:45. Then a peak around indices 38-45: up to 10.81? Actually, max near the end of the day: index 88: 14.30, which is around 22:00-22:15. So the highest peak is in the late evening. In reference Day 2, the highest peak is also in the evening: indices 88-91: 24.68, 25.32, 19.62... So the timing of the peak is similar. The morning activity is moderate. Overall shape is consistent with a scaled version of a typical weekday. Because the scaling factor varies somewhat across the day, I'd lean toward Attack 3: each sample multiplied by a different random factor. But could it be Attack 1 with a factor of about 0.6? The ratio min is 2.44/5.70≈0.43, but min is a single point and may be an outlier. The mean ratio is 6.93/11.53≈0.601. If we compute the ratio for each hour block? Not given. The standard deviation ratio is 2.84/5.01≈0.567. If it were exact scaling by a constant c, the ratio of means would be c, and ratio of std would be c, so c≈0.6 and 0.57, close. The difference could be due to the specific daily shape not being exactly proportional because the base load might not scale linearly? In electricity theft via meter bypass or magnetic interference, the reduction might be proportional to the current? Actually, physical attacks like magnetic interference or meter bypass often cause a percentage reduction that can be constant (Attack 1) or vary with current (Attack 3). False data injection could do either. Attack 3 is explicitly "different random factors per sample". Given that the query day's shape is very similar but not perfectly scaled uniformly, I think Attack 3 is the best match.

But let's consider Attack 2: on-off attack with zeros for random duration. There are no zeros, so it's not that.

Attack 4: random period reduced. The whole day is reduced, not just a period. So no.

Attack 6: cut-off. No evidence of many repeated max values. The max 14.30 appears only once? Actually, in query day list, 14.30 appears once, and 13.95 once. No clear ceiling. So no.

Attack 7: subtraction, but pattern doesn't fit a shift because night values would go to zero or negative if shift large enough to reduce peak that much.

Attack IEEE: some intervals zero, some normal. No zeros, so no.

Attack 12: swap with lower user. Lower user would likely have different timing habits. The query day timing matches typical residential with evening peak, so likely same household.

Thus, I'll classify as Manipulated, and the most likely attack is Attack 3.

Confidence: Medium to High? The evidence is strong that it's manipulated (significant reduction across the board). But distinguishing between Attack 1 and Attack 3 is subtle. Attack 3 explicitly says "different random factors per sample", while Attack 1 is a constant. The variation in ratio from night to day suggests non-constant factor, hence Attack 3. I'd say High confidence for manipulation, Medium for exact attack type (could be 1 with normal variation). But given the task, I'll go with Attack 3.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Identified Attack:

- Confidence: Medium
- Reasoning: The query day shows a drastic reduction in consumption (mean 16.75 vs. reference Sunday mean 45.84) with a slightly higher coefficient of variation (0.084 vs. 0.07), indicating both magnitude reduction and trend alteration. Attack 1 (constant scaling) was ruled out because the relative highs and lows do not align with the reference pattern; for instance, the reference peak at reading 87 (55.24) does not correspond to a high in the query day. Attack 5 (random multipliers on average consumption) was considered but ruled out because the observed mean (16.75) is far below the expected 27.8 under uniform(0,1) multipliers, making it statistically improbable (over 6 standard deviations). Attack 3 matches the profile of a low-magnitude, distorted pattern where each sample is multiplied by an independent random factor, consistent with the observed decoupling from the original shape and slightly increased relative variability.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Each 15-minute interval of the original consumption is multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1, drawn independently per sample. This results in a unique scaling for every reading, destroying the original temporal pattern while keeping values above zero.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall magnitude is significantly reduced (here to about 37% of the original mean), and the trend is altered because the per-sample random multipliers break the correlation structure of the genuine load profile, producing a flatter, more erratic series.
- Possible technical causes: Meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, or false data injection that applies a time-varying attenuation to the reported measurements.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Let's read the attack definitions carefully from the prompt:

Attack 0: replacing all with zeros.
Attack 1: multiplies by a constant random value between (0,1) - constant factor.
Attack 2: replacing with zeros for random duration (on-off).
Attack 3: multiplies by different random factors between 0 and 1, unique per sample.
Attack 4: simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: each sample replaced by a false value = avg normal consumption * random(0,1) per sample.
Attack 6: random cut-off point, values above replaced by that cut-off.
Attack 7: random cut-off point subtracted from each sample, negative -> zero.

Attack 3 changes both trend and amplitude, and the resulting pattern might appear as a low-magnitude, somewhat random-looking flat line. But Attack 1 with constant factor would have exactly the same CV as original (0.07), but here it's 0.084, a bit higher. So Attack 3's CV would be higher than original. So that might fit.

Attack 4 could also produce a similar effect if it applies a varying reduction. But given the definitions, Attack 3 explicitly mentions different factors per sample.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. 2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Comparing the sequences: The 0.5 scaling produces numbers like 19.535, 19.005, 20.065, 16.895, etc. The query values are mostly 19.73, 19.20, and 18.67. The query values don't exactly match 0.5 scaling; they seem to be discretized to a few distinct levels: 19.73, 19.20, 18.67, 17.60, 17.07, 16.53, 14.93, 14.40, 13.87, 13.33. These are not exactly half of the reference values. However, the pattern of ups and downs resembles the reference pattern but with a different factor applied. For example, early in reference: 28.51 -> query 19.73? Actually reference Day 4 first value 28.51, query first 19.73. 28.51 scaled by 0.692 would be 19.73. Next ref 39.07, query 19.73, that would be 0.505. Next ref 39.07, query 19.20, that's 0.491. So the scaling factor varies. This suggests Attack 3: each sample multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. But the factors are not entirely random; they seem to produce a compressed version of the profile. Alternatively, Attack 4: theft over a randomly selected period? But the entire day is reduced; no period is zero or original. Attack 2: on-off attack would have zeros for some durations. No zeros here. Attack 5: false values obtained by multiplying average normal consumption by random variable. Average normal consumption is about 36.6, multiplied by random 0-1 would produce values up to 36.6, but here max is 19.73, which is less. But it could be that the random variable is centered lower. Attack 5 says: "Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample." So it would be mean_ref * rand(). The mean_ref for Day 4 is 36.41. Multiplying by random 0-1 yields values from 0 to 36.41, but the query day values are clearly constrained between 13 and 20, not spanning the full range. Attack 6: cut-off: all values above a threshold replaced by threshold. If threshold is 19.73, then all high values would be 19.73, but many reference values are above 20, so they'd be capped. However, we see many values like 18.67, 19.20, 17.07, etc., which are below 19.73, so not all capped. Attack 7: subtract a cut-off; if result negative, zero. That would create zeros, not small positives. Attack 9: average daily consumption constant; not constant. Attack 10: reverse pattern, not reversed. Attack 12: replace with lower-consuming user pattern; possibly, but the pattern seems to mimic the shape of the original Day 4 but compressed. Attack IEEE: some intervals zeroed, no zeros.

The most consistent with the data is Attack 3: multiplication by different random factors per sample, or possibly Attack 1 with a single factor but the factor is not constant? Actually Attack 1 is "multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1)". That implies a single factor for the whole day. If it were a constant factor, the query values would be exactly ref*constant. The ratio of query/ref varies: first: 19.73/28.51=0.692; second: 19.73/39.07=0.505; third: 19.20/39.07=0.491; fourth: 19.73/38.01=0.519. So it's not constant. Thus Attack 3 is more likely: each sample multiplied by a different random factor. The variation in factors seems plausible: they chose factors that keep the consumption low but still follow the shape.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That might involve a part of the day being reduced and the rest normal. But here the entire day is reduced, no normal period. So Attack 4 would have some normal periods and some reduced periods; this day is uniformly low.

Attack 2: on-off would have zeros for random durations; no zeros.

Attack 5: average mean times random. If we take mean_ref=36.41, query values like 19.73 would be factor 0.54, 13.33 would be 0.366. The range is possible, but Attack 5 says the false value is obtained by multiplying average normal consumption by random variable. That means it disregards the shape entirely, generating random values around the mean. The query day still shows a pattern that roughly follows the reference day's ups and downs (e.g., dips at certain times). For instance, reference Day 4 has dips around indices where reference values drop to 27-28; query also has lower values (13-14) at similar times. So the pattern is preserved, not random. Therefore Attack 5 is unlikely.

Attack 6: cut-off. If we cap at 19.73, the reference values above 19.73 would become 19.73, but many reference values are above 19.73 and would be capped. In the query, we see 19.73 appearing often, but also many values below. However, the highest reference value is 40.13, which would be capped at 19.73; other high values like 39.07 would be capped. But we see query values of 19.20 and 18.67, which are below the cap. If the cap is 19.73, then any reference value above 19.73 becomes 19.73, while values below remain unchanged. But reference below 19.73? In reference, the minimum is 27.46, which is above 19.73. So all reference values are above 19.73! Wait, check reference Day 4: min is 27.46. So all values are >=27.46. If we cap at 19.73, all values would become 19.73. But query has many 19.20, 18.67, etc. So it can't be a simple cap. If the cut-off is lower, say 20, and then you replace exceeding values with the cut-off? Attack 6 says "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." If cut-off is 20, then all values >20 are replaced by 20. Then we'd see many 20s, but we see max 19.73, not exactly 20. And still, values would be either original (but original min is 27.46) or 20, so we'd see only 20 and values below? But no values are below 27.46 originally. So we'd see only 20 and maybe some values slightly above 20 if cut-off is higher? Actually if cut-off is 20, all values (since all >20) become 20, constant. Not matching. So Attack 6 is out.

Attack 7: subtract cut-off, if negative zero. Subtract say 20: 28.51-20=8.51, 39.07-20=19.07, etc. That would produce values like 8.5-20. So maybe cut-off around 20? Then we'd see values ranging from about 8 to 20, but query values are mostly 18-19, with min 13.33. Could be a cut-off around 20? 28.51-20=8.51, not 13.33; 27.46-20=7.46, not 13.33. So not matching exactly. Also, Attack 7 would produce some zeros for values below cut-off. No zeros here.

Attack 9: constant average; not constant.

Attack 10: reverse pattern; not reversed.

Attack 12: lower-consuming user. That would replace with a different pattern entirely. The query pattern looks like a scaled version of the same day, not a different user.

Attack IEEE: alternating zero and actual; no zeros.

Thus Attack 3 is the most likely: the pattern matches a scaled version of the reference day with varying scaling factors, which is defined as Attack 3 (multiplying by different random factors per sample). The magnitude is reduced by about 50% on average, and the trend is somewhat preserved but with some distortion. This can be caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current.

Now, confidence: high, because the pattern is clearly a compressed version of a typical day, consistent with per-sample scaling. The alternatives considered: Attack 1 (constant scaling) ruled out because ratio varies; Attack 4 (partial reduction) ruled out because entire day reduced; Attack 5 (random around mean) ruled out because pattern preservation; Attack 6/7 ruled out; Attack 2 no zeros.
